title="Plutoid">plutoids</a>"</td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Distant_minor_planets_sidebar" title="Template:Distant minor planets sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Distant_minor_planets_sidebar" title="Template talk:Distant minor planets sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Distant_minor_planets_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Kuiper belt</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/a&#618;/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">a&#618;</span><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/&#601;r/: \'er\' in \'letter\'">&#601;r</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> occasionally called the <b>Edgeworth&#8211;Kuiper belt</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disc" title="Circumstellar disc">circumstellar disc</a> in the outer <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, extending from the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> (at 30 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>) to approximately 50 AU from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, but is far larger&#8212;20 times as wide and 20 to 200 times as massive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Like the asteroid belt, it consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small bodies</a> or remnants from when the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Solar System formed</a>. While many asteroids are composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> and metal, most Kuiper belt objects are composed largely of frozen <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a> (termed "ices"), such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. The Kuiper belt is home to three officially recognized <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>:  <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>. Some of the Solar System\'s <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, such as Neptune\'s <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, may have originated in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Kuiper belt was named after Dutch-American astronomer <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a>, though he did not predict its existence. In 1992, <a href="/wiki/15760_Albion" title="15760 Albion">Albion</a> was discovered, the first Kuiper belt object (KBO) since Pluto and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qbee_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-qbee-7">[7]</a></sup> Since its discovery, the number of known KBOs has increased to over a thousand, and more than 100,000 KBOs over 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) in diameter are thought to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Kuiper belt was initially thought to be the main repository for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_comet" title="Periodic comet">periodic comets</a>, those with orbits lasting less than 200 years. Studies since the mid-1990s have shown that the belt is dynamically stable and that comets\' true place of origin is the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, a dynamically active zone created by the outward motion of Neptune 4.5&#160;billion years ago;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-9">[9]</a></sup> scattered disc objects such as <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> have extremely <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> orbits that take them as far as 100&#160;AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlutoSize_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlutoSize-12">[nb 1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Kuiper belt is distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">theoretical</a> <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, which is a thousand times more distant and is mostly spherical. The objects within the Kuiper belt, together with the members of the scattered disc and any potential <a href="/wiki/Hills_cloud" title="Hills cloud">Hills cloud</a> or Oort cloud objects, are collectively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> (TNOs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Pluto is the largest and most massive member of the Kuiper belt, and the largest and the second-most-massive known TNO, surpassed only by Eris in the scattered disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlutoSize_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlutoSize-12">[nb 1]</a></sup> Originally considered a planet, Pluto\'s status as part of the Kuiper belt caused it to be reclassified as a dwarf planet in 2006. It is compositionally similar to many other objects of the Kuiper belt and its orbital period is characteristic of a class of KBOs, known as "<a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>", that share the same 2:3 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Neptune.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Hypotheses"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hypotheses</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Discovery"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Discovery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Name"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Name</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Structure"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Structure</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Classical_belt"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Classical belt</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Resonances"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Resonances</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Kuiper_cliff"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Kuiper cliff</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Origin"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Origin</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Composition"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Composition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Mass_and_size_distribution"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Mass and size distribution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Scattered_objects"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Scattered objects</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Triton"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Triton</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Largest_KBOs"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Largest KBOs</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Pluto"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Pluto</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Satellites"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Satellites</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Exploration"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Exploration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Extrasolar_Kuiper_belts"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Extrasolar Kuiper belts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> in 1930, many speculated that it might not be alone. The region now called the Kuiper belt was hypothesized in various forms for decades. It was only in 1992 that the first direct evidence for its existence was found. The number and variety of prior speculations on the nature of the Kuiper belt have led to continued uncertainty as to who deserves credit for first proposing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015106_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015106-14">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses">Hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> to suggest the existence of a trans-Neptunian population was <a href="/wiki/Frederick_C._Leonard" title="Frederick C. Leonard">Frederick C. Leonard</a>. Soon after Pluto\'s discovery by <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a> in 1930, Leonard pondered whether it was "not likely that in Pluto there has come to light the <i>first</i> of a <i>series</i> of ultra-Neptunian bodies, the remaining members of which still await discovery but which are destined eventually to be detected".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> That same year, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Armin_Otto_Leuschner" title="Armin Otto Leuschner">Armin O. Leuschner</a> suggested that Pluto "may be one of many long-period planetary objects yet to be discovered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauch_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-lauch-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2833" data-file-width="1821" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg/170px-Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg/255px-Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg/340px-Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerard_Kuiper_1964b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a>, after whom the Kuiper belt is named</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1943, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_British_Astronomical_Association" title="Journal of the British Astronomical Association">Journal of the British Astronomical Association</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Edgeworth" title="Kenneth Edgeworth">Kenneth Edgeworth</a> hypothesized that, in the region beyond <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the material within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_element" title="Primordial element">primordial</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> was too widely spaced to condense into planets, and so rather condensed into a myriad of smaller bodies. From this he concluded that "the outer region of the solar system, beyond the orbits of the planets, is occupied by a very large number of comparatively small bodies"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> and that, from time to time, one of their number "wanders from its own sphere and appears as an occasional visitor to the inner solar system",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> becoming a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>.\n</p><p>In 1951, in a paper in <i>Astrophysics: A Topical Symposium</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a> speculated on a similar disc having formed early in the Solar System\'s evolution, but he did not think that such a belt still existed today. Kuiper was operating on the assumption, common in his time, that <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> was the size of Earth and had therefore scattered these bodies out toward the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> or out of the Solar System. Were Kuiper\'s hypothesis correct, there would not be a Kuiper belt today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The hypothesis took many other forms in the following decades. In 1962, physicist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alastair_GW_Cameron" title="Alastair GW Cameron">Al G.W. Cameron</a> postulated the existence of "a tremendous mass of small material on the outskirts of the solar system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2-20">[19]</a></sup> In 1964, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Whipple" title="Fred Whipple">Fred Whipple</a>, who popularised the famous "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dirty_snowball" title="Dirty snowball">dirty snowball</a>" hypothesis for cometary structure, thought that a "comet belt" might be massive enough to cause the purported discrepancies in the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> that had sparked the search for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planet_X" title="Planet X">Planet X</a>, or, at the very least, massive enough to affect the orbits of known comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Observation ruled out this hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2-20">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1977, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Kowal" title="Charles Kowal">Charles Kowal</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">2060 Chiron</a>, an icy planetoid with an orbit between Saturn and Uranus. He used a <a href="/wiki/Blink_comparator" title="Blink comparator">blink comparator</a>, the same device that had allowed <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a> to discover Pluto nearly 50 years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> In 1992, another object, <a href="/wiki/5145_Pholus" title="5145 Pholus">5145 Pholus</a>, was discovered in a similar orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Today, an entire population of comet-like bodies, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(planetoid)" title="Centaur (planetoid)">centaurs</a>, is known to exist in the region between Jupiter and Neptune. The centaurs\' orbits are unstable and have dynamical lifetimes of a few million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horner2004a_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horner2004a-24">[23]</a></sup> From the time of Chiron\'s discovery in 1977, astronomers have speculated that the centaurs therefore must be frequently replenished by some outer reservoir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Further evidence for the existence of the Kuiper belt later emerged from the study of comets. That comets have finite lifespans has been known for some time. As they approach the Sun, its heat causes their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatility_(physics)" title="Volatility (physics)">volatile</a> surfaces to sublimate into space, gradually dispersing them. In order for comets to continue to be visible over the age of the Solar System, they must be replenished frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-matter_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-matter-26">[25]</a></sup> One such area of replenishment is the Oort cloud, a spherical swarm of comets extending beyond 50,000 AU from the Sun first hypothesised by Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Jan_Oort" title="Jan Oort">Jan Oort</a> in 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> The Oort cloud is thought to be the point of origin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">long-period comets</a>, which are those, like <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Comet Hale&#8211;Bopp">Hale&#8211;Bopp</a>, with orbits lasting thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015105_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015105-28">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is another comet population, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short-period_comet" title="Short-period comet">short-period</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_comet" title="Periodic comet">periodic comets</a>, consisting of those comets that, like <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley\'s Comet">Halley\'s Comet</a>, have <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital periods</a> of less than 200 years. By the 1970s, the rate at which short-period comets were being discovered was becoming increasingly inconsistent with their having emerged solely from the Oort cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> For an Oort cloud object to become a short-period comet, it would first have to be <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_capture" title="Asteroid capture">captured</a> by the giant planets. In a paper published in <i><a href="/wiki/Monthly_Notices_of_the_Royal_Astronomical_Society" title="Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society">Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society</a></i> in 1980, Uruguayan astronomer <a href="/wiki/Julio_%C3%81ngel_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Julio &#193;ngel Fern&#225;ndez">Julio Fern&#225;ndez</a> stated that for every short-period comet to be sent into the inner Solar System from the Oort cloud, 600 would have to be ejected into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Interstellar_space" title="Outer space">interstellar space</a>. He speculated that a comet belt from between 35 and 50 AU would be required to account for the observed number of comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Following up on Fern&#225;ndez\'s work, in 1988 the Canadian team of Martin Duncan, Tom Quinn and <a href="/wiki/Scott_Tremaine" title="Scott Tremaine">Scott Tremaine</a> ran a number of computer simulations to determine if all observed comets could have arrived from the Oort cloud. They found that the Oort cloud could not account for all short-period comets, particularly as short-period comets are clustered near the plane of the Solar System, whereas Oort-cloud comets tend to arrive from any point in the sky. With a "belt", as Fern&#225;ndez described it, added to the formulations, the simulations matched observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> Reportedly because the words "Kuiper" and "comet belt" appeared in the opening sentence of Fern&#225;ndez\'s paper, Tremaine named this hypothetical region the "Kuiper belt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maunatele.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="163" data-file-width="385" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maunatele.jpg/250px-Maunatele.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maunatele.jpg/375px-Maunatele.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9e/Maunatele.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maunatele.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The array of telescopes atop <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea" title="Mauna Kea">Mauna Kea</a>, with which the Kuiper belt was discovered</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1987, astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Jewitt" title="David Jewitt">David Jewitt</a>, then at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>, became increasingly puzzled by "the apparent emptiness of the outer Solar System".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qbee_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-qbee-7">[7]</a></sup> He encouraged then-graduate student <a href="/wiki/Jane_Luu" title="Jane Luu">Jane Luu</a> to aid him in his endeavour to locate another object beyond <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>\'s orbit, because, as he told her, "If we don\'t, nobody will."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies3-33">[32]</a></sup> Using telescopes at the <a href="/wiki/Kitt_Peak_National_Observatory" title="Kitt Peak National Observatory">Kitt Peak National Observatory</a> in Arizona and the <a href="/wiki/Cerro_Tololo_Inter-American_Observatory" title="Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory">Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory</a> in Chile, Jewitt and Luu conducted their search in much the same way as Clyde Tombaugh and Charles Kowal had, with a <a href="/wiki/Blink_comparator" title="Blink comparator">blink comparator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies3_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies3-33">[32]</a></sup> Initially, examination of each pair of plates took about eight hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> but the process was sped up with the arrival of electronic <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled devices</a> or CCDs, which, though their field of view was narrower, were not only more efficient at collecting light (they retained 90% of the light that hit them, rather than the 10% achieved by photographs) but allowed the blinking process to be done virtually, on a computer screen. Today, CCDs form the basis for most astronomical detectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> In 1988, Jewitt moved to the Institute of Astronomy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">University of Hawaii</a>. Luu later joined him to work at the University of Hawaii\'s 2.24&#160;m telescope at <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea" title="Mauna Kea">Mauna Kea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Eventually, the field of view for CCDs had increased to 1024 by 1024 pixels, which allowed searches to be conducted far more rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Finally, after five years of searching, Jewitt and Luu announced on August 30, 1992 the "Discovery of the candidate Kuiper belt object" <a href="/wiki/15760_Albion" title="15760 Albion">15760 Albion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qbee_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-qbee-7">[7]</a></sup> Six months later, they discovered a second object in the region, <a href="/wiki/(181708)_1993_FW" title="(181708) 1993 FW">(181708) 1993 FW</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies conducted since the trans-Neptunian region was first charted have shown that the region now called the Kuiper belt is not the point of origin of short-period comets, but that they instead derive from a linked population called the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>. The scattered disc was created when Neptune <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">migrated outward</a> into the proto-Kuiper belt, which at the time was much closer to the Sun, and left in its wake a population of dynamically stable objects that could never be affected by its orbit (the Kuiper belt proper), and a population whose <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelia</a> are close enough that Neptune can still disturb them as it travels around the Sun (the scattered disc). Because the scattered disc is dynamically active and the Kuiper belt relatively dynamically stable, the scattered disc is now seen as the most likely point of origin for periodic comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-book-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Astronomers sometimes use the alternative name Edgeworth&#8211;Kuiper belt to credit Edgeworth, and KBOs are occasionally referred to as EKOs. <a href="/wiki/Brian_G._Marsden" title="Brian G. Marsden">Brian G. Marsden</a> claims that neither deserves true credit: "Neither Edgeworth nor Kuiper wrote about anything remotely like what we are now seeing, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Whipple" title="Fred Whipple">Fred Whipple</a> did".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> David Jewitt comments: "If anything... Fern&#225;ndez most nearly deserves the credit for predicting the Kuiper Belt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>KBOs are sometimes called "kuiperoids", a name suggested by <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian object</a>" (TNO) is recommended for objects in the belt by several scientific groups because the term is less controversial than all others&#8212;it is not an exact <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonym</a> though, as TNOs include all objects orbiting the Sun past the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, not just those in the Kuiper belt.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv/512px--Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="166.53351473923" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Dust_Models_Paint_Alien&amp;#039;s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv" type="video/ogg;
