codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="239624" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="307624" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv.en.srt&amp;amp;action=raw&amp;amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="en" label="English (en) subtitles" data-mwtitle="TimedText:Dust_Models_Paint_Alien&amp;#039;s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv.en.srt" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-dir="ltr"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Dust Models Paint Alien\'s View of Solar System.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv/220px--Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dust_Models_Paint_Alien%27s_View_of_Solar_System.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">Dust</a> in the Kuiper belt creates a faint <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> disc. (Click on the "play" button to watch the video.)</div></div></div>\n<p>At its fullest extent (but excluding the scattered disc), including its outlying regions, the Kuiper belt stretches from roughly 30 to 55 AU. The main body of the belt is generally accepted to extend from the 2:3 mean-motion resonance (<a href="#Resonances">see below</a>) at 39.5 AU to the 1:2 resonance at roughly 48 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The Kuiper belt is quite thick, with the main concentration extending as much as ten degrees outside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_of_the_ecliptic" title="Plane of the ecliptic">ecliptic plane</a> and a more diffuse distribution of objects extending several times farther. Overall it more resembles a <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a> or doughnut than a belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Its mean position is inclined to the ecliptic by 1.86 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The presence of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> has a profound effect on the Kuiper belt\'s structure due to <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a>. Over a timescale comparable to the age of the Solar System, Neptune\'s gravity destabilises the orbits of any objects that happen to lie in certain regions, and either sends them into the inner Solar System or out into the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> or interstellar space. This causes the Kuiper belt to have pronounced gaps in its current layout, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gaps</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>. In the region between 40 and 42 AU, for instance, no objects can retain a stable orbit over such times, and any observed in that region must have migrated there relatively recently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7500" data-file-width="12000" decoding="async" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png/550px-KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png/825px-KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png/1100px-KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png 2x" width="550"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KBO_diagram_eccentricity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The various dynamical classes of trans-Neptunian objects.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_belt">Classical belt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classical belt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">Classical Kuiper belt object</a></div>\n<p>Between the 2:3 and 1:2 resonances with Neptune, at approximately 42&#8211;48&#160;AU, the gravitational interactions with Neptune occur over an extended timescale, and objects can exist with their orbits essentially unaltered. This region is known as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">classical Kuiper belt</a>, and its members comprise roughly two thirds of KBOs observed to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Because the first modern KBO discovered (<a href="/wiki/15760_Albion" title="15760 Albion">Albion</a>, but long called (15760) 1992 QB<sub>1</sub>), is considered the prototype of this group, classical KBOs are often referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubewanos" title="Cubewanos">cubewanos</a> ("Q-B-1-os").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Committee_on_Small_Body_Nomenclature" title="Committee on Small Body Nomenclature">guidelines</a> established by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAU" title="IAU">IAU</a> demand that classical KBOs be given names of mythological beings associated with creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clas_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-clas-49">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The classical Kuiper belt appears to be a composite of two separate populations. The first, known as the "dynamically cold" population, has orbits much like the planets; nearly circular, with an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> of less than 0.1, and with relatively low inclinations up to about 10&#176; (they lie close to the plane of the Solar System rather than at an angle). The cold population also contain a concentration of objects, referred to as the kernel, with semi-major axes at 44&#8211;44.5 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petit_2011_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petit_2011-50">[49]</a></sup> The second, the "dynamically hot" population, has orbits much more inclined to the ecliptic, by up to 30&#176;. The two populations have been named this way not because of any major difference in temperature, but from analogy to particles in a gas, which increase their relative velocity as they become heated up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2003_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2003-51">[50]</a></sup> Not only are the two populations in different orbits, the cold population also differs in color and <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, being redder and brighter, has a larger fraction of binary objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephen_Noll_2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephen_Noll_2006-52">[51]</a></sup> has a different size distribution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraser_etal_2014_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser_etal_2014-53">[52]</a></sup> and lacks very large objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison_Stern_2001_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levison_Stern_2001-54">[53]</a></sup> The difference in colors may be a reflection of different compositions, which suggests they formed in different regions. The hot population is proposed to have formed near Neptune\'s original orbit and to have been scattered out during the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">migration</a> of the giant planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2005_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2005-55">[54]</a></sup> The cold population, on the other hand, has been proposed to have formed more or less in its current position because the loose binaries would be unlikely to survive encounters with Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_etal_2011a_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker_etal_2011a-56">[55]</a></sup> Although the Nice model appears to be able to at least partially explain a compositional difference, it has also been suggested the color difference may reflect differences in surface evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2008-57">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resonances">Resonances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Resonances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">Resonant trans-Neptunian object</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KBOs_and_resonances.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6044" data-file-width="13860" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/KBOs_and_resonances.png/220px-KBOs_and_resonances.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/KBOs_and_resonances.png/330px-KBOs_and_resonances.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/KBOs_and_resonances.png/440px-KBOs_and_resonances.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KBOs_and_resonances.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubewano" title="Cubewano">cubewanos</a> (blue), <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">Resonant trans-Neptunian objects</a> (red), <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">Sednoids</a> (yellow) and <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered objects</a> (grey)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/220px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/330px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/440px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Orbit classification (schematic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>When an object\'s orbital period is an exact ratio of Neptune\'s (a situation called a <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">mean-motion resonance</a>), then it can become locked in a synchronised motion with Neptune and avoid being perturbed away if their relative alignments are appropriate. If, for instance, an object orbits the Sun twice for every three Neptune orbits, and if it reaches perihelion with Neptune a quarter of an orbit away from it, then whenever it returns to perihelion, Neptune will always be in about the same relative position as it began, because it will have completed &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> orbits in the same time. This is known as the 2:3 (or 3:2) resonance, and it corresponds to a characteristic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> of about 39.4&#160;AU. This 2:3 resonance is populated by about 200 known objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">its moons</a>. In recognition of this, the members of this family are known as <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>. Many plutinos, including Pluto, have orbits that cross that of Neptune, though their resonance means they can never collide. Plutinos have high orbital eccentricities, suggesting that they are not native to their current positions but were instead thrown haphazardly into their orbits by the migrating Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trojan_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-trojan-59">[58]</a></sup> IAU guidelines dictate that all plutinos must, like Pluto, be named for underworld deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clas_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-clas-49">[48]</a></sup> The 1:2 resonance (whose objects complete half an orbit for each of Neptune\'s) corresponds to semi-major axes of ~47.7AU, and is sparsely populated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> Its residents are sometimes referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twotino" title="Twotino">twotinos</a>. Other resonances also exist at 3:4, 3:5, 4:7 and 2:5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Neptune has a number of <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">trojan objects</a>, which occupy its <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>, gravitationally stable regions leading and trailing it in its orbit. Neptune trojans are in a 1:1 mean-motion resonance with Neptune and often have very stable orbits.\n</p><p>Additionally, there is a relative absence of objects with semi-major axes below 39 AU that cannot apparently be explained by the present resonances. The currently accepted hypothesis for the cause of this is that as Neptune migrated outward, unstable orbital resonances moved gradually through this region, and thus any objects within it were swept up, or gravitationally ejected from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kuiper_cliff"><span id=".22Kuiper_cliff.22"></span>Kuiper cliff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Kuiper cliff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="644" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg/220px-Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg/330px-Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg/440px-Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hot_and_cold_KBO.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Histogram of the semi-major axes of Kuiper belt objects with inclinations above and below 5&#160;degrees. Spikes from the plutinos and the &#8216;kernel&#8217; are visible at 39&#8211;40&#160;AU and 44&#160;AU.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 1:2 resonance, location of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twotino" title="Twotino">twotinos</a> at 47.8&#160;AU, appears to be an edge beyond which few objects are known. It is not clear whether it is actually the outer edge of the classical belt or just the beginning of a broad gap. Objects have been detected at the 2:5&#160;resonance at roughly 55&#160;AU, well outside the classical belt; predictions of a large number of bodies in classical orbits between these resonances have not been verified through observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trojan_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-trojan-59">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Based on estimations of the primordial mass required to form <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and Neptune, as well as bodies as large as Pluto <i>(see <a href="#Mass_and_size_distribution">&#167;&#160;Mass and size distribution</a>)</i>, earlier models of the Kuiper belt had suggested that the number of large objects would increase by a factor of two beyond 50&#160;AU,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_1999_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_1999-63">[62]</a></sup> so this sudden drastic falloff, known as the <i>Kuiper cliff</i>, was unexpected, and to date its cause is unknown. In 2003, Bernstein, Trilling, et al. found evidence that the rapid decline in objects of 100&#160;km or more in radius beyond 50&#160;AU is real, and not due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observational_bias" title="Observational bias">observational bias</a>. Possible explanations include that material at that distance was too scarce or too scattered to accrete into large objects, or that subsequent processes removed or destroyed those that did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_et_al._2004_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_et_al._2004-64">[63]</a></sup> Patryk Lykawka of <a href="/wiki/Kobe_University" title="Kobe University">Kobe University</a> claimed that the gravitational attraction of an <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">unseen large planetary object</a>, perhaps the size of Earth or <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, might be responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lhborbits.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/400px-Lhborbits.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/600px-Lhborbits.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/800px-Lhborbits.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lhborbits.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simulation showing outer planets and Kuiper belt: a) before Jupiter/Saturn 1:2 resonance, b) scattering of Kuiper belt objects into the Solar System after the orbital shift of Neptune, c) after ejection of Kuiper belt bodies by Jupiter</div></div></div>\n<p>The precise origins of the Kuiper belt and its complex structure are still unclear, and astronomers are awaiting the completion of several wide-field survey telescopes such as <a href="/wiki/Pan-STARRS" title="Pan-STARRS">Pan-STARRS</a> and the future <a href="/wiki/Large_Synoptic_Survey_Telescope" title="Large Synoptic Survey Telescope">LSST</a>, which should reveal many currently unknown KBOs. These surveys will provide data that will help determine answers to these questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Kuiper belt is thought to consist of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>, fragments from the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" title="Protoplanetary disc">protoplanetary disc</a> around the Sun that failed to fully coalesce into planets and instead formed into smaller bodies, the largest less than 3,000 kilometres (1,900&#160;mi) in diameter. Studies of the crater counts on Pluto and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> revealed a scarcity of small craters suggesting that such objects formed directly as sizeable objects in the range of tens of kilometers in diameter rather than being accreted from much smaller, roughly kilometer scale bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Hypothetical mechanisms for the formation of these larger bodies include the gravitational collapse of clouds of pebbles concentrated between eddies in a turbulent protoplanetary disk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_etal_2011a_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parker_etal_2011a-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuzzi_etal_2010_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuzzi_etal_2010-68">[67]</a></sup> or in <a href="/wiki/Streaming_instability" title="Streaming instability">streaming instabilities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johansen_Jacquet_2015_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johansen_Jacquet_2015-69">[68]</a></sup> These collapsing clouds may fragment, forming binaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_etal_2010a_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_etal_2010a-70">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">computer simulations</a> show the Kuiper belt to have been strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, and also suggest that neither <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> nor Neptune could have formed in their present positions, because too little primordial matter existed at that range to produce objects of such high mass. Instead, these planets are estimated to have formed closer to Jupiter. Scattering of planetesimals early in the Solar System\'s history would have led to <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">migration</a> of the orbits of the giant planets: <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, Uranus, and Neptune drifted outwards, whereas Jupiter drifted inwards. Eventually, the orbits shifted to the point where Jupiter and Saturn reached an exact 1:2 resonance; Jupiter orbited the Sun twice for every one Saturn orbit. The gravitational repercussions of such a resonance ultimately destabilized the orbits of Uranus and Neptune, causing them to be scattered outward onto high-eccentricity orbits that crossed the primordial planetesimal disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2008_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2008-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsiganis05_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsiganis05-72">[71]</a></sup> While Neptune\'s orbit was highly eccentric, its mean-motion resonances overlapped and the orbits of the planetesimals evolved chaotically, allowing planetesimals to wander outward as far as Neptune\'s 1:2 resonance to form a dynamically cold belt of low-inclination objects. Later, after its eccentricity decreased, Neptune\'s orbit expanded outward toward its current position. Many planetesimals were captured into and remain in resonances during this migration, others evolved onto higher-inclination and lower-eccentricity orbits and escaped from the resonances onto stable orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Many more planetesimals were scattered inward, with small fractions being captured as Jupiter trojans, as irregular satellites orbiting the giant planets, and as outer belt asteroids. The remainder were scattered outward again by Jupiter and in most cases ejected from the Solar System reducing the primordial Kuiper belt population by 99% or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2008_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2008-57">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original version of the currently most popular model, the "<a href="/wiki/Nice_model#Formation_of_the_Kuiper_belt" title="Nice model">Nice model</a>", reproduces many characteristics of the Kuiper belt such as the "cold" and "hot" populations, resonant objects, and a scattered disc, but it still fails to account for some of the characteristics of their distributions. The model predicts a higher average eccentricity in classical KBO orbits than is observed (0.10&#8211;0.13 versus 0.07) and its predicted inclination distribution contains too few high inclination objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2008_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2008-57">[56]</a></sup> In addition, the frequency of binary objects in the cold belt, many of which are far apart and loosely bound, also poses a problem for the model. These are predicted to have been separated during encounters with Neptune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_Kavelaars_2010_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker_Kavelaars_2010-74">[73]</a></sup> leading some to propose that the cold disc formed at its current location, representing the only truly local population of small bodies in the solar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Five-planet_Nice_model" title="Five-planet Nice model">recent modification</a> of the Nice model has the Solar System begin with five giant planets, including an additional <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giant</a>, in a chain of mean-motion resonances. About 400 million years after the formation of the Solar System the resonance chain is broken. Instead of being scattered into the disc, the ice giants first migrate outward several AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_Morbidelli_2012_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_Morbidelli_2012-76">[75]</a></sup> This divergent migration eventually leads to a resonance crossing, destabilizing the orbits of the planets. The extra ice giant encounters Saturn and is scattered inward onto a Jupiter-crossing orbit and after a series of encounters is ejected from the Solar System. The remaining planets then continue their migration until the planetesimal disc is nearly depleted with small fractions remaining in various locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_Morbidelli_2012_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_Morbidelli_2012-76">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As in the original Nice model, objects are captured into resonances with Neptune during its outward migration. Some remain in the resonances, others evolve onto higher-inclination, lower-eccentricity orbits, and are released onto stable orbits forming the dynamically hot classical belt. The hot belt\'s inclination distribution can be reproduced if Neptune migrated from 24 AU to 30 AU on a 30 Myr timescale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_2015a_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_2015a-77">[76]</a></sup> When Neptune migrates to 28 AU, it has a gravitational encounter with the extra ice giant. Objects captured from the cold belt into the 1:2 mean-motion resonance with Neptune are left behind as a local concentration at 44 AU when this encounter causes Neptune\'s semi-major axis to jump outward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_2015b_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_2015b-78">[77]</a></sup> The objects deposited in the cold belt include some loosely bound \'blue\' binaries originating from closer than the cold belt\'s current location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraser_etal_2017_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser_etal_2017-79">[78]</a></sup> If Neptune\'s eccentricity remains small during this encounter, the chaotic evolution of orbits of the original Nice model is avoided and a primordial cold belt is preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff_etal_2012_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff_etal_2012-80">[79]</a></sup> In the later phases of Neptune\'s migration, a slow sweeping of mean-motion resonances removes the higher-eccentricity objects from the cold belt, truncating its eccentricity distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli_etal_2014_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli_etal_2014-81">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/200px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/300px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/400px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The infrared spectra of both Eris and Pluto, highlighting their common methane absorption lines</div></div></div>\n<p>Being distant from the Sun and major planets, Kuiper belt objects are thought to be relatively unaffected by the processes that have shaped and altered other Solar System objects; thus, determining their composition would provide substantial information on the makeup of the earliest Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup> Due to their small size and extreme distance from Earth, the chemical makeup of KBOs is very difficult to determine. The principal method by which astronomers determine the composition of a celestial object is <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>. When an object\'s light is broken into its component colors, an image akin to a rainbow is formed. This image is called a <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectrum</a>. Different substances absorb light at different wavelengths, and when the spectrum for a specific object is unravelled, dark lines (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_line" title="Absorption line">absorption lines</a>) appear where the substances within it have absorbed that particular wavelength of light. Every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Element_(chemistry)" title="Element (chemistry)">element</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_(chemistry)" title="Compound (chemistry)">compound</a> has its own unique spectroscopic signature, and by reading an object\'s full spectral "fingerprint", astronomers can determine its composition.\n</p><p>Analysis indicates that Kuiper belt objects are composed of a mixture of rock and a variety of ices such as water, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. The temperature of the belt is only about 50 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quaoar_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quaoar-83">[82]</a></sup> so many compounds that would be gaseous closer to the Sun remain solid. The densities and rock&#8211;ice fractions are known for only a small number of objects for which the diameters and the masses have been determined. The diameter can be determined by imaging with a high-resolution telescope such as the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, by the timing of an <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> when an object passes in front of a star or, most commonly, by using the <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of an object calculated from its infrared emissions. The masses are determined using the semi-major axes and periods of satellites, which are therefore known only for a few binary objects. The densities range from less than 0.4 to 2.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup>. The least dense objects are thought to be largely composed of ice and have significant porosity. The densest objects are likely composed of rock with a thin crust of ice. There is a trend of low densities for small objects and high densities for the largest objects. One possible explanation for this trend is that ice was lost from the surface layers when differentiated objects collided to form the largest objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3971" data-file-width="5981" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg/220px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg/330px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg/440px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exiled_asteroid_2004_EW95.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist&#8217;s impression of plutino and possible former <a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">C-type asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/(120216)_2004_EW95" title="(120216) 2004 EW95">2004 EW<sub>95</sub></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Initially, detailed analysis of KBOs was impossible, and so astronomers were only able to determine the most basic facts about their makeup, primarily their color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KBOKBO_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-KBOKBO-85">[84]</a></sup> These first data showed a broad range of colors among KBOs, ranging from neutral grey to deep red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-color_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-color-86">[85]</a></sup> This suggested that their surfaces were composed of a wide range of compounds, from dirty ices to <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-color_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-color-86">[85]</a></sup> This diversity was startling, as astronomers had expected KBOs to be uniformly dark, having lost most of the volatile ices from their surfaces to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Various solutions were suggested for this discrepancy, including resurfacing by impacts or <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KBOKBO_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-KBOKBO-85">[84]</a></sup> Jewitt and Luu\'s spectral analysis of the known Kuiper belt objects in 2001 found that the variation in color was too extreme to be easily explained by random impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> The radiation from the Sun is thought to have chemically altered methane on the surface of KBOs, producing products such as <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> has been shown to possess a number of hydrocarbons derived from the radiation-processing of methane, including <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although to date most KBOs still appear spectrally featureless due to their faintness, there have been a number of successes in determining their composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quaoar_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quaoar-83">[82]</a></sup> In 1996, Robert H. Brown et al. acquired spectroscopic data on the KBO 1993 SC, which revealed that its surface composition is markedly similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, as well as Neptune\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, with large amounts of methane ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rbrown_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-rbrown-89">[88]</a></sup> For the smaller objects, only colors and in some cases the albedos have been determined. These objects largely fall into two classes: gray with low albedos, or very red with higher albedos. The difference in colors and albedos is hypothesized to be due to the retention or the loss of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (H<sub>2</sub>S) on the surface of these objects, with the surfaces of those that formed far enough from the Sun to retain H<sub>2</sub>S being reddened due to irradiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wong_Brown_2016_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wong_Brown_2016-90">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest KBOs, such as Pluto and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaoar" title="Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, have surfaces rich in volatile compounds such as methane, <a href="/wiki/Solid_nitrogen" title="Solid nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>; the presence of these molecules is likely due to their moderate vapor pressure in the 30&#8211;50&#160;K temperature range of the Kuiper belt. This allows them to occasionally boil off their surfaces and then fall again as snow, whereas compounds with higher boiling points would remain solid. The relative abundances of these three compounds in the largest KBOs is directly related to their <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">surface gravity</a> and ambient temperature, which determines which they can retain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup> Water ice has been detected in several KBOs, including members of the Haumea family such as <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(19308)_1996_TO66" title="(19308) 1996 TO66">1996 TO<sub>66</sub></a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> mid-sized objects such as <a href="/wiki/38628_Huya" title="38628 Huya">38628 Huya</a> and <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">20000 Varuna</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> and also on some small objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup> The presence of crystalline ice on large and mid-sized objects, including <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">50000 Quaoar</a> where <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrate</a> has also been detected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quaoar_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Quaoar-83">[82]</a></sup> may indicate past tectonic activity aided by melting point lowering due to the presence of ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_2012a_82-5"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2012a-82">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_and_size_distribution">Mass and size distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mass and size distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg/250px-TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg/375px-TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg/500px-TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_PowerLaw2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the power law</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite its vast extent, the collective <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the Kuiper belt is relatively low. The total mass is estimated to range between 1/25 and 1/10 the <a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass">mass of the Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g01_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-g01-93">[92]</a></sup> Conversely, models of the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Solar System\'s formation</a> predict a collective mass for the Kuiper belt of 30 Earth masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup> This missing &gt;99% of the mass can hardly be dismissed, because it is required for the accretion of any KBOs larger than 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) in diameter. If the Kuiper belt had always had its current low density, these large objects simply could not have formed by the collision and mergers of smaller planetesimals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup> Moreover, the eccentricity and inclination of current orbits makes the encounters quite "violent" resulting in destruction rather than accretion. It appears that either the current residents of the Kuiper belt have been created closer to the Sun, or some mechanism dispersed the original mass. Neptune\'s current influence is too weak to explain such a massive "vacuuming", though the <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a> proposes that it could have been the cause of mass removal in the past. Although the question remains open, the conjectures vary from a passing star scenario to grinding of smaller objects, via collisions, into dust small enough to be affected by solar radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2005_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2005-55">[54]</a></sup> The extent of mass loss by collisional grinding is limited by the presence of loosely bound binaries in the cold disk, which are likely to be disrupted in collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny_etal_2011a_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny_etal_2011a-94">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bright objects are rare compared with the dominant dim population, as expected from accretion models of origin, given that only some objects of a given size would have grown further. This relationship between <i>N</i>(<i>D</i>) (the number of objects of diameter greater than <i>D</i>) and <i>D</i>, referred to as brightness slope, has been confirmed by observations. The slope is inversely proportional to some power of the diameter <i>D</i>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {dN}{dD}}\\propto D^{-q}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>N</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8733;<!-- &#8733; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>q</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {dN}{dD}}\\propto D^{-q}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\frac {dN}{dD}}\\propto D^{-q}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9508bf212d3bf23316c22ee9eb412373f86028f" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.405ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span> where the current measures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_et_al._2004_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_et_al._2004-64">[63]</a></sup> give q = 4 &#177;0.5.</dd></dl>\n<p>This implies (assuming <i>q</i> is not 1) that\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N\\propto D^{1-q}+{\\text{a constant  }}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mo>&#8733;<!-- &#8733; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>q</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>a constant&#160;</mtext>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N\\propto D^{1-q}+{\\text{a constant  }}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle N\\propto D^{1-q}+{\\text{a constant  }}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ea235adc1c609bc51fa3e2bfbe5376eb2f87fbae" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:24.654ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>(The constant may be non-zero only if the power law doesn\'t apply at high values of <i>D</i>.)\n</p><p>Less formally, if <i>q</i> is 4, for example, there are 8 (=2<sup>3</sup>) times more objects in the 100&#8211;200&#160;km range than in the 200&#8211;400&#160;km range, and for every object with a diameter between 1000 and 1010&#160;km there should be around 1000 (=10<sup>3</sup>) objects with diameter of 100 to 101&#160;km.\n</p><p>If <i>q</i> was 1 or less, the law would imply an infinite number and mass of large objects in the Kuiper belt. If 1&lt;<i>q</i>&#8804;4 there will be a finite number of objects greater than a given size, but the <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> of their combined mass would be infinite. If <i>q</i> is 4 or more, the law would imply an infinite mass of small objects. More accurate models find that the "slope" parameter <i>q</i> is in effect greater at large diameters and lesser at small diameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_et_al._2004_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_et_al._2004-64">[63]</a></sup> It seems that <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> is somewhat unexpectedly large, having several percent of the total mass of the Kuiper belt. It is not expected that anything larger than Pluto exists in the Kuiper belt, and in fact most of the brightest (largest) objects at inclinations less than 5&#176; have probably been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_et_al._2004_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_et_al._2004-64">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For most TNOs, only the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> is actually known, the size is inferred assuming a given <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> (not a safe assumption for larger objects).\n</p><p>Recent research has revealed that the size distributions of the hot classical and cold classical objects have differing slopes. The slope for the hot objects is q = 5.3 at large diameters and q = 2.0 at small diameters with the change in slope at 110&#160;km. The slope for the cold objects is q = 8.2 at large diameters and q = 2.9 at small diameters with a change in slope at 140&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraser_etal_2014_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser_etal_2014-53">[52]</a></sup> The size distributions of the scattering objects, the plutinos, and the Neptune trojans have slopes similar to the other dynamically hot populations, but may instead have a divot, a sharp decrease in the number of objects below a specific size. This divot is hypothesized to be due to either the collisional evolution of the population, or to be due to the population having formed with no objects below this size, with the smaller objects being fragments of the original objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shankman_etal_2016a_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shankman_etal_2016a-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexandersen_etal_2014a_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexandersen_etal_2014a-96">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of December 2009, the smallest Kuiper belt object detected is 980&#160;m across. It is too dim (<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> 35) to be seen by <i>Hubble</i> directly, but it was detected by <i>Hubble\'</i>s star tracking system when it <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scattered_objects">Scattered objects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scattered objects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg/240px-TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg/360px-TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg/480px-TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_Projections_100AU_Classical_SDO.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of the orbits of scattered disc objects (black), classical KBOs (blue), and 2:5 resonant objects (green). Orbits of other KBOs are gray. (Orbital axes have been aligned for comparison.)</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">Scattered disc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">Centaur (minor planet)</a></div>\n<p>The scattered disc is a sparsely populated region, overlapping with the Kuiper belt but extending to beyond 100 AU. Scattered disc objects (SDOs) have very elliptical orbits, often also very inclined to the ecliptic. Most models of Solar System formation show both KBOs and SDOs first forming in a primordial belt, with later gravitational interactions, particularly with Neptune, sending the objects outward, some into stable orbits (the KBOs) and some into unstable orbits, the scattered disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-book-9">[9]</a></sup> Due to its unstable nature, the scattered disc is suspected to be the point of origin of many of the Solar System\'s short-period comets. Their dynamic orbits occasionally force them into the inner Solar System, first becoming <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a>, and then short-period comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-book-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>, which officially catalogues all trans-Neptunian objects, a KBO, strictly speaking, is any object that orbits exclusively within the defined Kuiper belt region regardless of origin or composition. Objects found outside the belt are classed as scattered objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cen_sdo_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-cen_sdo-98">[97]</a></sup> In some scientific circles the term "Kuiper belt object" has become synonymous with any icy minor planet native to the outer Solar System assumed to have been part of that initial class, even if its orbit during the bulk of Solar System history has been beyond the Kuiper belt (e.g. in the scattered-disc region). They often describe scattered disc objects as "scattered Kuiper belt objects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, which is known to be more massive than Pluto, is often referred to as a KBO, but is technically an SDO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cen_sdo_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-cen_sdo-98">[97]</a></sup> A consensus among astronomers as to the precise definition of the Kuiper belt has yet to be reached, and this issue remains unresolved.\n</p><p>The centaurs, which are not normally considered part of the Kuiper belt, are also thought to be scattered objects, the only difference being that they were scattered inward, rather than outward. The Minor Planet Center groups the centaurs and the SDOs together as scattered objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cen_sdo_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-cen_sdo-98">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p><br/>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triton">Triton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Triton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton (moon)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_(large).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4700" data-file-width="4700" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/170px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/255px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/340px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_(large).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During its period of migration, Neptune is thought to have captured a large KBO, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, which is the only large moon in the Solar System with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a> (it orbits opposite to Neptune\'s rotation). This suggests that, unlike the large <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">moons of Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">Saturn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">Uranus</a>, which are thought to have coalesced from rotating discs of material around their young parent planets, Triton was a fully formed body that was captured from surrounding space. Gravitational capture of an object is not easy: it requires some mechanism to slow down the object enough to be caught by the larger object\'s gravity. A possible explanation is that Triton was part of a binary when it encountered Neptune. (Many KBOs are members of binaries. See <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt#Satellites" title="Kuiper belt">below</a>.) Ejection of the other member of the binary by Neptune could then explain Triton\'s capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Triton is only 14% larger than Pluto, and spectral analysis of both worlds shows that their surfaces are largely composed of similar materials, such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>. All this points to the conclusion that Triton was once a KBO that was captured by Neptune during its <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">outward migration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_KBOs">Largest KBOs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Largest KBOs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_brightest_Kuiper_belt_objects" title="List of the brightest Kuiper belt objects">List of the brightest Kuiper belt objects</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><div class="noresize"><map title="Salacia"/><area alt="File:EightTNOs.png" coords="0,0,310,225" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" shape="rect" title="File:EightTNOs.png"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage"
