usemap="#ImageMap_1_1237649146" width="310"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artistic comparison of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" title="(307261) 2002 MS4">2002 MS<sub>4</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" title="120347 Salacia">Salacia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> along with the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini" style="float:bottom"><span style="margin-right:-0.125em">[ </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TNO_imagemap" title="Template:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TNO_imagemap" title="Template talk:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TNO_imagemap&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul><span style="margin-left:-0.125em"> ]</span></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Since 2000, a number of KBOs with diameters of between 500 and 1,500&#160;km (932&#160;mi), more than half that of Pluto (diameter 2370&#160;km), have been discovered. <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">50000 Quaoar</a>, a classical KBO discovered in 2002, is over 1,200&#160;km across. <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, both announced on July 29, 2005, are larger still. Other objects, such as <a href="/wiki/28978_Ixion" title="28978 Ixion">28978 Ixion</a> (discovered in 2001) and <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">20000 Varuna</a> (discovered in 2000), measure roughly 500&#160;km (311&#160;mi) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto">Pluto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pluto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a></div>\n<p>The discovery of these large KBOs in orbits similar to Pluto\'s led many to conclude that, aside from its relative size, <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> was not particularly different from other members of the Kuiper belt. Not only are these objects similar to Pluto in size, but many also have <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">satellites</a>, and are of similar composition (methane and carbon monoxide have been found both on Pluto and on the largest KBOs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-3">[3]</a></sup> Thus, just as <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> was considered a planet before the discovery of its fellow <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, some began to suggest that Pluto might also be reclassified.\n</p><p>The issue was brought to a head by the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, an object in the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> far beyond the Kuiper belt, that is now known to be 27% more massive than Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> (Eris was originally thought to be larger than Pluto by volume, but the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> mission found this not to be the case.) In response, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) was forced to <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">define what a planet is</a> for the first time, and in so doing included in their definition that a planet must have "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighborhood" title="Clearing the neighborhood">cleared the neighbourhood</a> around its orbit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> As Pluto shares its orbit with many other sizable objects, it was deemed not to have cleared its orbit, and was thus reclassified from a planet to a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>, making it a member of the Kuiper belt.\n</p><p>Although Pluto is currently the largest known KBO, there is at least one known larger object currently outside the Kuiper belt that probably originated in it: Neptune\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> (which, as explained above, is probably a captured KBO).\n</p><p>As of 2008, only five objects in the Solar System (Ceres, Eris, and the KBOs Pluto, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>) are listed as dwarf planets by the IAU. <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">90482 Orcus</a>, 28978 Ixion and <a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets" title="List of possible dwarf planets">many other Kuiper-belt objects</a> are large enough to be in hydrostatic equilibrium; most of them will probably qualify when more is known about them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-albedo_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-albedo-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites">Satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The six largest TNOs (Eris, Pluto, <span class="nowrap"> 2007 OR<sub>10</sub></span>, Makemake, Haumea and Quaoar) are all known to have satellites, and two have more than one. A higher percentage of the larger KBOs have satellites than the smaller objects in the Kuiper belt, suggesting that a different formation mechanism was responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> There are also a high number of binaries (two objects close enough in mass to be orbiting "each other") in the Kuiper belt. The most notable example is the Pluto&#8211;Charon binary, but it is estimated that around 11% of KBOs exist in binaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1118" data-file-width="1118" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/225px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/338px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/450px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The KBO <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> (green circles), the selected target for the <i>New Horizons</i> Kuiper belt object mission</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_MU69_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/2014_MU69_orbit.jpg/200px-2014_MU69_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/2014_MU69_orbit.jpg/300px-2014_MU69_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/2014_MU69_orbit.jpg/400px-2014_MU69_orbit.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_MU69_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing the location of <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> and trajectory for rendezvous</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UltimaThule_colorized.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UltimaThule_colorized.png/220px-UltimaThule_colorized.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UltimaThule_colorized.png/330px-UltimaThule_colorized.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UltimaThule_colorized.png/440px-UltimaThule_colorized.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UltimaThule_colorized.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i> image of <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> revealing its red color, suggesting organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190210_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190210-111">[110]</a></sup> So far, it is the only KBO besides Pluto and its satellites to be visited by a spacecraft.</div></div></div>\n<p>On January 19, 2006, the first spacecraft to explore the Kuiper belt, <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>, was launched, which flew by <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> on July 14, 2015. Beyond the Pluto flyby, the mission\'s goal was to locate and investigate other, farther objects in the Kuiper belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 15, 2014, it was revealed that <i>Hubble</i> had uncovered three potential targets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141015_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141015-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall2014_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall2014-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> provisionally designated PT1 ("potential target 1"), PT2 and PT3 by the <i>New Horizons</i> team. The objects\' diameters were estimated to be in the 30&#8211;55&#160;km range; too small to be seen by ground telescopes, at distances from the Sun of 43&#8211;44 AU, which would put the encounters in the 2018&#8211;2019 period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-114">[113]</a></sup> The initial estimated probabilities that these objects were reachable within <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> fuel budget were 100%, 7%, and 97%, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-114">[113]</a></sup> All were members of the "cold" (low-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclination</a>, low-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt" title="Classical Kuiper belt">classical Kuiper belt</a>, and thus very different from Pluto. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">PT1</a> (given the temporary designation "1110113Y" on the HST web site<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>), the most favorably situated object, was magnitude 26.8, 30&#8211;45&#160;km in diameter, and will be encountered around January 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VOX-20150414_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-VOX-20150414-119">[118]</a></sup> Once sufficient orbital information was provided, the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> gave official designations to the three target KBOs: <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> (PT1), <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_OS393" title="2014 OS393">2014 OS<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">393</span></span></a></span> (PT2), and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_PN70" title="2014 PN70">2014 PN<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">70</span></span></a></span> (PT3). By the fall of 2014, a possible fourth target, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_MT69" title="2014 MT69">2014 MT<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span>, had been eliminated by follow-up observations. PT2 was out of the running before the Pluto flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 26, 2015, the first target, <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span>, was chosen. Course adjustment took place in late October and early November 2015, leading to a flyby in January 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> On July 1, 2016, NASA approved additional funding for <i>New Horizons</i> to visit the object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 2, 2015, <i>New Horizons</i> detected <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1994_JR1" title="1994 JR1">1994 JR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">1</span></span></a></span> from 270&#160;million kilometres (170<span style="margin-left:0.2em">&#215;<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;mi) away, and the photographs show the shape of the object and one or two details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 1, 2019, <i>New Horizons</i> successfully flew by <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>, returning data showing <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> to be a contact binary 32 km long by 16 km wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190210_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190210-111">[110]</a></sup> The Ralph instrument confirmed <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>\'s red color. Data from the fly by will continue to be downloaded over the next 20 months. \n</p><p>No follow up missions for <i>New Horizons</i> are planned, though at least two concepts for missions that would return to orbit or land on Pluto have been studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> Beyond Pluto, there exist many large KBOs that cannot be visited with <i>New Horizons</i>, such as the dwarf planets <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>. New missions would be tasked to explore and study these objects in detail. <a href="/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space" title="Thales Alenia Space">Thales Alenia Space</a> has studied the logistics of an orbiter mission to Haumea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> a high priority scientific target due to its status as the parent body of a collisional family that includes several other TNOs, as well as Haumea\'s ring and two moons. The lead author, Joel Poncy, has advocated for new technology that would allow spacecraft to reach and orbit KBOs in 10&#8211;20 years or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> Principal Investigator Alan Stern has informally suggested missions that would flyby the planets Uranus or Neptune before visiting new KBO targets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> thus furthering the exploration of the Kuiper belt while also visiting these <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giant</a> planets for the first time since the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> flybys in the 1980s.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a> would make a particularly attractive flyby target for a probe tasked with exploring the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, as it currently lies near the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliospheric</a> nose; Pontus Brandt at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a> <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> and his colleagues have studied a probe that would flyby Quaoar in the 2030s before continuing to the intersellar medium through the heliospheric nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Quaoar is also an attractive target due to a likely disappearing methane atmosphere and <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryo-volcanism</a>. The mission studied by Brandt and his colleagues would launch using <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">SLS</a> and achieve 30&#160;km/s using a Jupiter flyby. Alternatively, for an orbiter mission, a study published in 2012 concluded that <a href="/wiki/28978_Ixion" title="28978 Ixion">Ixion</a> and <a href="/wiki/38628_Huya" title="38628 Huya">Huya</a> are among the most feasible targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> For instance, the authors calculated that an orbiter mission could reach Ixion after 17 years cruise time if launched in 2039.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extrasolar_Kuiper_belts">Extrasolar Kuiper belts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Extrasolar Kuiper belts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debris_disc" title="Debris disc">Debris disc</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1524" data-file-width="2908" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg/250px-Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg/375px-Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg/500px-Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_remote.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Debris discs around the stars <a href="/wiki/HD_139664" title="HD 139664">HD 139664</a> and <a href="/wiki/HD_53143" title="HD 53143">HD 53143</a> &#8211; black circle from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronograph" title="Coronograph">camera</a> hides star to display discs.</div></div></div>\n<p>By 2006, astronomers had resolved dust discs thought to be Kuiper belt-like structures around nine stars other than the Sun. They appear to fall into two categories: wide belts, with radii of over 50 AU, and narrow belts (tentatively like that of the Solar System) with radii of between 20 and 30 AU and relatively sharp boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalas_et_al._2006_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalas_et_al._2006-133">[132]</a></sup> Beyond this, 15&#8211;20% of solar-type stars have an observed <a href="/wiki/Infrared_excess" title="Infrared excess">infrared excess</a> that is suggestive of massive Kuiper-belt-like structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> Most known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debris_disc" title="Debris disc">debris discs</a> around other stars are fairly young, but the two images on the right, taken by the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> in January 2006, are old enough (roughly 300 million years) to have settled into stable configurations. The left image is a "top view" of a wide belt, and the right image is an "edge view" of a narrow belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalas_et_al._2006_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kalas_et_al._2006-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> Computer simulations of dust in the Kuiper belt suggest that when it was younger, it may have resembled the narrow rings seen around younger stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><ul style="font-weight: bold">\n<li><a width="17"/></a></span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Solar_System" title="Portal:Solar System">Solar System portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-PlutoSize-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-PlutoSize_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-PlutoSize_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The literature is inconsistent in the usage of the terms <i>scattered disc</i> and <i>Kuiper belt</i>. For some, they are distinct populations; for others, the scattered disc is part of the Kuiper belt. Authors may even switch between these two uses in one publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Because the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>, the body responsible for cataloguing <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a> in the Solar System, makes the distinction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> the editorial choice for Wikipedia articles on the trans-Neptunian region is to make this distinction as well. On Wikipedia, Eris, the most-massive known trans-Neptunian object, is not part of the Kuiper belt and this makes Pluto the most-massive Kuiper belt object.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuiper_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.oxforddictionaries.com/definition/english/Kuiper-belt" rel="nofollow">Kuiper belt &#8211; oxforddictionaries.com</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Stern, Alan; Colwell, Joshua E. (1997). href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1997ApJ...490..879S" rel="nofollow">1997ApJ...490..879S</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1086%2F304912" rel="nofollow">10.1086/304912</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988"
